1
Dynamic D2D-Assisted Federated Learning over
O-RAN: Performance Analysis, MAC Scheduler,
and Asymmetric User Selection
Payam Abdisarabshali, Student Member, IEEE, Kwang Taik Kim, Senior Member, IEEE,
Michael Langberg, Fellow, IEEE, Weifeng Su, Fellow, IEEE, and Seyyedali Hosseinalipour, Senior Member, IEEE
Abstract—Existing studies on federated learning (FL) are mostly
focused on system orchestration for static snapshots of the net-
work and making static control decisions (e.g., spectrum allocation).
However, real-world wireless networks are susceptible to temporal
variations of wireless channel capacity and users’ datasets. In
this paper, we study the impacts of the dynamics of (i) wireless
channels and (ii) users’ datasets on the FL execution. The former
is captured by introducing a set of discrete time events while the
latter is characterized by a novel ordinary differential equation
and the metric of dynamic model drift, formulated via a par-
tial differential inequality, drawing concrete analytical connections
between the dynamics of users’ datasets and FL accuracy. We
then propose dynamic cooperative FL with dedicated MAC
schedulers (DCLM), exploiting the unique features of open radio
access network (O-RAN) to execute FL. DCLM entails (i) a
hierarchical device-to-device (D2D)-assisted model training, (ii)
dynamic control decisions through dedicated O-RAN MAC sched-
ulers, and (iii) asymmetric user selection. We provide extensive
theoretical analysis to study the convergence of DCLM and then
aim to optimize its degrees of freedom (e.g., user selection and
spectrum allocation) through a non-convex optimization problem.
We develop a systematic and generic approach to obtain the
solution for this problem. We finally show the efficiency of DCLM
via numerical simulations and provide a series of future directions.
Index Terms—Federated learning, system dynamics, user
selection, open RAN, MAC scheduler, performance analysis.
I. INTRODUCTION
F
EDERATED learning (FL) is an emerging distributed
machine learning (ML) paradigm that enables mobile
users to collaboratively train models without sharing raw data,
making it valuable for various Internet-of-Things (IoT) appli-
cations, such as smart healthcare and autonomous driving [1].
FL involves model training through multiple global rounds,
each comprising: (i) a server broadcasts an ML model, called
global model (GM), to users; (ii) users perform local model
(LM) training using their data and the received GM (e.g., via
the stochastic gradient descent (SGD)); (iii) users transfer their
trained LMs to the server, and the server aggregates the received
LMs (e.g., via weighted averaging) into a new GM.
P. Abdisarabshali, M. Langberg, W. Su, and S. Hosseinalipour are with the
Department of Electrical Engineering, University at Buffalo-SUNY, NY, USA
(emails: {payamabd, mikel, weifeng, alipour}@buffalo.edu). K. T. Kim is with
the department of Electrical and Computer Engineering, Purdue University,
IN, USA (email:kimkt@purdue.edu))
This work was supported by the U.S. National Science Foundation (NSF)
under Grant ECCS 2512911.
Corresponding Author: S. Hosseinalipour
A. Motivation and Solution Overview
FL is expected to be implemented on resource-constrained
devices (e.g., smartphones and IoT sensors) [2], posing chal-
lenges due to modern ML models’ large parameter sizes, which
demand significant local computation power and incur high
communication overhead during model transfers. Motivated
by this, research has focused on (i) handling device’ compu-
tation/communication heterogeneities [3], [4]; (ii) optimizing
resource (e.g., spectrum) allocation [2], [5]–[7] (iii) optimizing
the periodicity of client-to-server communications [5], [8]; (iv)
smart client sampling [6], [8]; and (v) leveraging inter-user
cooperation to reduce training costs (e.g., energy usage) [5].
Despite the significance of the above works, they share a
common yet restrictive assumption, which we term snapshot-
based analysis: they assume that users’ datasets and client-
to-server wireless channels remain static throughout each
global round of FL. Consequently, they perform snapshot-
based analysis of the network, treating each snapshot as a
representation of the network and users at the beginning of
each global round. They then perform control decisions, such
as spectrum allocation and user recruitment for each snapshot.
However, such snapshot-based analysis undermines the
practicality of the existing studies, as in real-world scenar-
ios, wireless channels and user datasets experience temporal
fluctuations during each FL global round, demanding adaptive
ML and wireless control strategies. In this paper, for the first
time, we relax this key assumption by incorporating (i) dynamic
wireless channels of users and (ii) dynamic datasets of users
into FL. We capture the former by introducing a set of events
that occur at discrete time instants, which we refer to as D-
Events. To capture the later, we develop two models. First, we
model the dynamics of each user’s dataset, in terms of dataset
size, via an ordinary differential equation. Second, through
a partial differential inequality, we introduce a new metric
called dynamic model drift to measure the impact of temporal
variation of users’ datasets on FL accuracy. We demonstrate
theoretically that these dynamics impact the resource allocation
decisions, user sampling/recruitment, training latency, and
energy consumption, as well as FL model convergence. These
theoretical results highlight the importance of performing
dynamic resource allocation – a task conducted by MAC
schedulers of radio access networks (RANs) – tailored to
the requirements of FL. Based on these findings, we introduce
dynamic cooperative FL with dedicated MAC schedulers
arXiv:2404.06324v2  [cs.NI]  25 Oct 2025
2
(DCLM), which will be deployed at RAN, to perform dynamic
resource (e.g., spectrum/power) allocation and user recruitment.
We note that traditional RANs, such as distributed RAN used
in 4G networks rely on black-box MAC schedulers to perform
dynamic spectrum/power allocation for all types of users [9].
These black-box schedulers are proprietary, meaning only the
hardware vendors can modify them, which limits the network’s
flexibility and adaptability to diverse service requirements. In
the context of FL, effective resource management must account
for FL-specific factors, such as model prediction accuracy and
client recruitment costs, alongside QoS metrics such as energy
consumption and model training latency. These FL-specific
considerations and limitations of traditional RANs in addressing
them motivate us to develop our DCLM over Open RAN (O-
RAN), an emerging RAN used in 5G-and-beyond networks.
O-RAN (see Fig. 1) disaggregates elements (i.e., 3GPP
protocol stack) of each base station (BS) into (i) radio unit
(O-RU), (ii) distributed unit (O-DU), and (iii) centralized unit
(O-CU) [10]. O-RAN provides a software-based infrastructure
that allows the design of network functionalities tailored
to the specific resource requirements of various services,
a feature known as programmability. For instance, users
with ultra-reliable low-latency communication requirement can
benefit from a tailored MAC scheduler. O-RAN enables this
level of flexibility through (i) RAN slicing (creating virtual
RANs for each service) and (ii) RAN intelligent controllers,
which host applications known as rApps and xApps. These
applications define network management policies based on
service requirements of users of each slice and the network
conditions (e.g., channel gains). A detailed explanation on the
evolution of RAN is provided in Appendix F. In this paper, we
formulate DCLM as an optimization that can be encapsulated
into rApps and xApps and deployed at O-RAN to establish
MAC scheduling and user recruitment policies tailored to the
requirements of FL (e.g., FL model accuracy), dynamics of
wireless channels, and time-varying datasets of users.
DCLM orchestrates FL at two levels of agility through
breaking down each FL global round into multiple smaller
time instants, called fine granular time instants (FGTIs). In
particular, (i) ML-related control decisions are performed at
each global round, e.g., recruiting FLUs, and (ii) dynamic
wireless spectrum/power allocation are performed at each FGTI.
B. Related Work
1) FL and O-RAN: To predict data traffic of O-RAN slices,
[11] proposes a slicing-based user selection FL algorithm.
Further, a hierarchical FL-based resource allocation scheme
and network slicing for O-RAN has been proposed in [12].
However, these works differ from ours since they utilize FL for
O-RAN orchestration while we use the potentials of O-RAN to
facilitate the execution of FL in dynamic wireless networks.
2) MAC schedulers: Authors in [13] propose AdaSlicing, an
adaptive O-RAN slicing framework with real-time AI-driven
orchestration. Further, [14] proposes an O-RAN scheduling
framework that uses inter-slice control via xApps and slice-
aware MAC scheduling to meet users’ service level agreements.
Extending this literature, we are the first to design dedicated
MAC schedulers for the execution of FL over O-RAN.

Fig. 1: O-RAN architecture and integration of dedicated FL-related functional-
ities. O-RAN components interact via standard open interfaces, labeled with
E2, F1, open fronthaul, A1, and O1 interfaces, which facilitate interoperability
between network elements from different manufacturers.
3) User recruitment: Several studies [6], [8] investigate
improving the efficiency of FL via proper user selection.
Specifically, [8] chooses users that exhibit a low degree of
data heterogeneity and [6] groups users with similar data types
under one user to achieve near i.i.d. data distribution. Our work
contributes to this literature by proposing a methodology for
FL user recruitment over O-RAN under system dynamics.
4) FL system orchestration: Authors in [5] proposed an FL
system orchestrator aimed at minimizing energy consumption
via spectrum allocation and device-to-device (D2D) communi-
cations. Another research [15] employs a sequential wireless
resource scheduling by establishing a queue to determine the
order in which users transmit their models to the server. To
our knowledge, the existing FL orchestrations, including those
mentioned, neither consider O-RAN nor orchestrate FL under
system dynamics, which we achieve both in this work.
5) Decentralized FL: Authors in [16] propose a gossip
approach for decentralized FL, where each user partitions its
large ML model and shares different segments with neighboring
users during FL training rounds. Similarly, [17] highlights the
effectiveness of gossip learning under uniform data distribution
across the clients. Our work introduces the first D2D-assisted
FL orchestration framework over O-RAN, which extends the
existing decentralized FL approaches by proposing multi-
timescale system orchestration and dedicated MAC schedulers
while accounting for fine-granular system dynamics.
C. Summary of Contributions
Our main contributions can be summarized as follows:
•
We incorporate the dynamics of wireless channels of FL
users (FLUs) and their datasets into FL orchestration. To
this end, we first introduce a discrete-time model comprising
a set of D-Events. Afterwards, we model the dynamics of
each FLU’s dataset size as an ordinary differential equation
and introduce a metric named dynamic model drift, which is
modeled as a partial differential inequality. These analytical

Fig. 2: The FL MAC scheduler establishes multi-channel connections between
DPUs, CHUs, and O-RUs. DCLM uses DCC to perform a hierarchical ML

models draw one of the first connections between the impact
of temporal variation of FLUs’ datasets and FL accuracy.
•
Utilizing the potential of O-RAN, we introduce DCLM, a
D2D-assisted multi time-scale system orchestrator for FL
operating at two levels of agility: (i) global FL training
rounds (coarse time-scale) and (ii) FGTIs (fine time-scale).
•
In DCLM, we introduce (i) the dynamic hierarchical FL, (ii)
an asymmetric FLU recruitment strategy, and (iii) dispersed
collaborative/cooperative communication (DCC) for FL.
•
Leveraging the programmability of O-RAN, we design
dedicated MAC schedulers for DCLM to conduct dynamic
spectrum and transmit power allocation for FLU-to-O-
RU (i.e., uplink/downlink) and FLU-to-FLU (i.e., D2D)
communications tailored to dynamic wireless channel and
datasets of FLUs during FGTIs.
•
We conduct extensive theoretical analysis of DCLM and
derive a set of new FL convergence bounds.
•
We provide both theoretical and experimental answers to
the following fundamental open research questions:
(Q1) How dynamic wireless channel, time-varying datasets of
FLUs, non-i.i.d. FLUs’ datasets, and the dissimilarity
of data points within the dataset of an FLU jointly
influence decisions regarding FLU recruitment?
(Q2) What is the impact of dynamic wireless control de-
cisions on (i) the FL model performance, (ii) model
training latency, and (iii) energy consumption?
•
We demonstrate the efficiency of DCLM in terms of ML
model performance and network resource savings via
numerical simulations considering real-world ML datasets.
II. SYSTEM MODEL AND DESIGN CONSIDERATION
Below, we describe the main aspects of our system model.
The major notations and acronyms used in the paper are listed
in Table III and Table IV in Appendix A. Also, one of our
goals is to introduce a variety of future directions for this
underexplored field of research, which is done in Appendix B.
A. System Architecture
Referring to Fig. 2, we consider an FL service1 [18]
performed by a set of geo-distributed FLUs across various
O-RUs in O-RAN, gathered by set Ω. The model training
is coordinated by a server through a sequence of global
rounds k ∈K = {0, · · · , K}. The set of FLUs within
the coverage of each O-RU b∈Ωis collected via Ub. We
assume a prior knowledge on the presence of FLUs in
the system, supported by prior works [2], [4], [5], [15].
Further extensions to stochastic FLU arrival and departure
are discussed in Appendix B. We consider heterogeneous
communication/computation capabilities across FLUs, where
an FLU u∈Ub has a maximum transmit power of P max
u
and
time-varying available CPU frequency of f (k)
u .
We consider data transmissions scheduled in group(s) of 12
sub-carriers, known as physical resource blocks (PRBs). To
support heterogeneous services, 5G new radio adopts a set
of numerologies [19] that tune the bandwidth of PRBs. Each
numerology is characterized by an integer γ, enforcing the
subcarrier spacing to be 2γ ×15 KHz, where 15 KHz is the ref-
erence subcarrier spacing. In this paper, we consider two types
of PRBs, including (i) licensed PRBs used for communication
between O-RUs and FLUs and (ii) unlicensed PRBs exploited
for device-to-device (D2D) communications (i.e., FLU-to-FLU
communications). We refer to the bandwidth of licensed and
unlicensed PRBs of each O-RU by B=12×15×2γ1 KHz and
B=12×15×2γ2 KHz, respectively, where γ1 and γ2 are their
respective numerologies. Moreover, we consider a realistic
scenario where multiple PRBs can be assigned to each user,
enabling high-speed communications.
We consider a RAN slice for FL at each O-RU b, provisioned
with a maximum downlink transmit power of P max
b
. Further,
we presume the worst-case scenario where FL slices of all
O-RUs have access to the same sets of licensed and unlicensed
PRBs denoted by Rb and Rb, b∈Ω. Thus, FLUs experience (i)
intra-cell interference from other FLUs in the same O-RU that
use the same PRBs and (ii) inter-cell interference from FLUs
using the same PRBs in nearby O-RUs. This calls for adaptive
interference management and transmit power control, which
we develop in this work.2 We model both downlink and uplink
communications for FL. In the downlink, the server broadcasts
the GM to recruited FLUs via licensed PRBs of O-RUs. In
the uplink, to upload FLUs’ LMs, we propose the dispersed
collaborative/cooperative communication (DCC) mode (see
Fig. 2), splitting FLUs into (i) communication head FLUs
(CHUs) and (ii) deprived FLUs (DPUs). In particular, DPUs
disperse unequal fractions of their LMs to multiple CHUs via
low-power D2D communications on unlicensed PRBs instead
of communicating directly with O-RUs. Afterward, CHUs
aggregate their LMs with those received from DPUs into a
single LM, which is transferred to O-RUs via licensed PRBs.
DCC conserves DPUs’ resources (e.g., transmit power) and
network resources (e.g., licensed PRBs), as shown in Sec. VIII.
1For research directions on the coexistence of multiple FL services with
conflicting interests refer to Appendix B.
2Research directions on elastic RAN slicing (i.e., scale down/up of resources
provisioned to O-RAN slices) are provided in Appendix B.

B. System Dynamic
In DCLM, we migrate from the snapshot-based analysis of FL,
described in Sec. I, by taking the first step toward incorporating
system dynamics into FL, which in turn calls for dynamic
control decisions. Specifically, to capture the system dynamics
in DCLM, for each global round k∈K, let [T (k−1), T (k)] be a
wall-clock time window (measured in seconds), where T (k)
denotes the time when global round k is finished and T (−1)=0.
We consider two types of multi-granular dynamics that impact
the performance of FL: (M1) discrete-time dynamic FLUs’
resource requirements (e.g., wireless spectrum) and (M2)
continuous-time dynamic FLUs’ local datasets. Below, we
focus on (M1) and model (M2) later in Sec. III-A.
Discrete-time dynamic FLUs’ resource requirements. For
brevity, we refer to the processes associated with transmitting
ML models (i.e., uploading LMs of FLUs and downloading
the GM) as communication tasks (CTs). Further, we define the
following variables: (i) τ ↓,(k)
b
: Broadcast (downlink) latency
of O-RU b to transmit the GM to the recruited FLUs over
the licensed PRBs; (ii) τ LC,(k)
u

time of FLU u∈Ub; (iii) τ ↑,(k)
u

u∈Ub over the unlicensed PRBs; (iv) τ W,(k)

CHU u∈Ub for receiving all LMs from its associated DPUs
in DCC; (v) τ ↑,(k)
u
: LM upload latency of CHU u∈Ub over
the licensed PRBs. Accordingly, below, we introduce a set
of discrete-time events, called D-Events, which cover all the
discrete-time events in which the resource requirement of the
users may experience a change.3 We further define a function
Ψ(·)∈[T (k−1), T (k)), which takes a D-Event as an input and
outputs its occurrence time.

D-Events build the pillars of analysis of the system dynamics
in DCLM. These events can cause fluctuations in the resource
requirements of FL slices during model training. For example,
occurrence of D↓,(k)
u
can increase the licensed PRB requirement
of FL slices, while D
↚
,(k)
u
may decrease it. D-Events further
motivate us to incorporate a novel notion into FL, where
virtualization and RAN slicing in O-RAN provide a unique
platform for harnessing D-Events. This notion is dynamic MAC
scheduler, which allocates resources (e.g., PRB and transmit
power) of each virtually crafted RAN slice to the FLUs. Details
of formalizing this notion will be presented in Sec. IV.
While D-Events make the modeling more realistic, they
significantly increase the complexity of controlling network
elements for FL. DCLM addresses this complexity by employing
a multi-time scale management system. Specifically, in DCLM,
we split the duration of each global round k (i.e., [T (k−1), T (k)))
into multiple fine granular time instants (FGTIs) to tackle sys-
tem dynamics caused by D-Events, allowing for a continuum of
decisions (e.g., dynamic PRB allocation by MAC schedulers).
Formally, we divide [T (k−1), T (k)) into multiple FGTIs and in-
troduce decision variable tx∈[T (k−1), T (k)) to refer to a specific
FGTI. Here, x∈N (k), where N (k)={(N (k−1)+1), · · ·, N (k)}, is
the set of N (k)−N (k−1)>0 integer values (i.e., the indices of
FGTIs); N (−1) = 0. Also tx+1 > tx, ∀x ∈∪k∈KN (k) \ {N (K)}.
The impact of tx on system performance and optimization of
its occurrence time are presented in Secs. IV&V and VII.
C.
Integration of DCLM into O-RAN
O-RAN enables the development of dedicated MAC sched-
ulers through non-real-time RAN intelligent controllers (Non-
RT RICs), which define network management policies. In
particular, data (e.g., users’ positions), generated by users
and the RAN nodes (e.g., O-RUs) stream periodically via
the O1 interface to the virtual event streaming (VES). The
collected data is utilized to build non-RT applications known
as rApps (see Fig. 1), which handle long-term tasks such as
training ML models and solving optimization problems. These
rApps will be packaged into applications known as xApps
and deployed on near-RT RICs. A key use case of xApp is
defining resource allocation policy of the MAC schedulers at
O-DUs. Specifically, unlike black box MAC schedulers used
in traditional RANs (e.g., distributed RAN) for all services,
xApps offer a flexible white box, software-based infrastructure
for designing a dedicated MAC scheduler tailored to a specific
network service. In DCLM, we formulate a dedicated MAC
scheduler for FL as an optimization problem in Sec. VII, which
will be packaged within an rApp in non-RT RIC (see Fig. 1).
The solution of this problem will subsequently be packaged into
an xApp to define MAC scheduling policy. This MAC scheduler
manages three tasks: (i) scheduling O-RUs at different FGTIs
and assigning licensed PRBs for broadcasting the GM to FLUs;
(ii) managing D2D operations by scheduling DPUs at different
FGTIs and allocating unlicensed PRBs to the scheduled DPUs
for dispersing their LMs across CHUs; and (iii) scheduling
CHUs at different FGTIs and allocating licensed PRBs to the
5
scheduled CHUs for transmitting their aggregated LMs to O-
RUs. To facilitate understanding, we visualize the protocol flow
of DCLM in Fig. 10 in Appendix D, illustrating the interactions
among the DCLM MAC scheduler, O-RUs, CHUs, and DPUs.
In the following, we present the steps involved in DCLM.
III. DCLM DISTRIBUTED MACHINE LEARNING MODEL
In DCLM, each global round k ∈K engages/recruits a subset of
FLUs (including DPUs and CHUs). To determine the recruit-
ment status of FLU u, we utilize bλ(k)

u +λ
(k)
u ≤1.
In DCLM, the following seven steps are conducted in each
global round k. (s-i) Broadcasting a GM: the server broadcasts
a GM, characterized by model parameter ω(k) ∈RM, to the re-
cruited FLUs through O-RUs. (s-ii) LM training: each recruited
CHUs and DPUs initializes its LM with the received GM and
starts training its LM using its dataset via stochastic gradient
descent (SGD). (s-iii) Dispersion of gradient vector (GV) of
DPUs: each recruited DPU disperses unequal fractions/parts
of its GV among CHUs (optimized in Sec. IV-B). (s-iv) GV
aggregation at CHUs: each recruited CHU aggregates the GVs
received from its associated DPUs and its own GV into one
GV. (s-v) Dispatching of GVs of CHUs: each recruited CHU
uploads different fractions of its aggregated GV to an O-RU
over multiple PRBs (CHU to O-RU assignment and PRB
allocation are formulated in Sec. IV-B). (s-vi) GV aggregation
at O-RUs: each O-RU aggregates the received GVs from its
associated CHUs into one GV and transfers it to the server.
(s-vii) GM aggregation: the server aggregates the received GVs
from O-RUs to a new GM ω(k+1), utilized for round k+1.
Below, we formulate different aspects of DCLM: dynamics of
FLUs’ local datasets (Sec. III-A), distributed training objective
of DCLM (Sec. III-B), LM training (Sec. III-C), LM dispersion
by DPUs (Sec. III-D), and aggregation procedure (Sec. III-E).
A. Continuous-Time Dynamic FLUs’ Local Datasets
In contrast to the majority of works on FL that assume FLUs’
datasets are static [2], [5]–[7], we consider a realistic scenario
where FLUs’ datasets change over time when not engaged in
model training. Let T train,(k)

We next use a measure called model drift to capture how
the dynamics of datasets formulated in Sec. III-A1 affect the
accuracy of an ML model in terms of local loss Lu(ω|Υu(t)).
3) Dynamic model drift: Few works on FL have looked into
model drift [20] (e.g., to capture the variation of data between
two consecutive global rounds). However, none of them can
be applied to DCLM as the continuous-time dynamics of FLUs’
datasets (Sec. III-A1) lead to a continuous-time variation of
data throughout the entire FL processes (i.e., within [0, T (K)]).
Motivated by this, we introduce a new definition for model
drift, which is in the form of a partial differential inequality.
This allows us to accurately quantify the impact of fluctuations
in the data distribution during each global round k. Our later
analysis in Sec. VI will highlight the impact of this metric on
the performance of DCLM and reveal a set of novel insights.
Definition 1 (Dynamic Model Drift). Let ω(k)∈RM be a GM
with M elements. We formulate dynamic model drift of FLU
u at global round k via a partial differential inequality:
∂
∂t
|Υu(t)|
|Υ(t)| Lu(ω(k)|Υu(t))

≤Du(t), t∈T idle,(k)
u
.
(3)
As model drift value Du(t) increases, it becomes difficult
to track the best model parameters since the loss function
rapidly increases. Moreover, (3) also includes situations where
the old model becomes more fitting for current data when
Du(t)<0. The definition in (3) generalizes the discrete model
drift in [20] and extend it to capture the rate of change in
model performance over continuous time, allowing for the use
of continuous time function approximations in capturing the
model drift. This is particularly suitable for applications that
generate streaming data with rapidly evolving distributions [21].
Considering the above system dynamics, in DCLM, we aim
to perform dynamic ML training as described below.
6
B.
Distributed Training Objective of DCLM
At the k-th global round, let L(k)
u (ω)≜Lu(ω|Υu(T (k))) denote
the local loss of FLU u and L(k)(ω)≜L(ω|Υ(T (k))) represent
the global loss for an ML model ω at time T (k). Due to
the dynamics of local datasets, the optimal global model is
time-varying and forms a sequence

ω(k)⋆	K
k=1, where ω(k)⋆=
arg min L(k)(ω), ω ∈RM, ∀k ∈K. To track this sequence while
taking into account the energy consumption and training latency
of FL, DCLM leverages O-RAN programmability and introduces
a dedicated MAC scheduler for FL to perform optimal dynamic
resource (i.e., PRB and transmit power) allocation. Note that, as
dynamic model drift Du(t) across FLUs increases, the training
procedure (including LM training and model transmitting) must
be conducted faster to track the optimal global model. Also,
devices with larger values of Du(t) should be recruited more
frequently since neglecting them will lead to having an outdated
global model. These intuitions are later concretized and verified
by our theoretical analysis in Sec. VI.

E. Temporal and Hierarchical FL Model Aggregation
Considering DCC depicted in Fig. 2, DCLM establishes a
4-tier uplink communication hierarchy. The server is situated
at the top (layer 1), O-RUs are located at layer 2, followed
by CHUs at layer 3, and DPUs at layer 4. Subsequently, at
each global round k, the process of uploading local GVs in
DCLM occurs in three hierarchical stages, starting with DPUs
to CHUs, then from CHUs to O-RUs, and finally from O-
RUs to the server. In this scheme, components of each layer
utilize a designated aggregation strategy to aggregate GVs
received from the lower layer into a single GV, which will
be transmitted to the upper layer. Specifically, as explained
below, the aggregation process starts with performing dynamic
fragmented local GVs aggregation at CHUs during FGTIs of
global round k (i.e., layer 3).8 Then, integrated local GVs
aggregation is done at O-RUs (i.e., layer 2). Lastly, global
model aggregation is performed at the server (i.e., layer 1).
1) Dynamic fragmented local GVs aggregation: Each CHU
u aggregates its own GV and the GVs received from its DPUs
into an aggregated GV as follows:

IV. DYNAMIC WIRELESS CONTROL DECISIONS
To orchestrate the procedure outlined in the previous section
while taking into account the latency and energy overhead
of FL in a dynamic environment, dynamic wireless control
decisions become essential. However, D-Events complicate
these decisions by perturbing the instantaneous resource
demands of FLUs. To address this challenge, DCLM formalizes
a suite of dynamic wireless control decisions (Fig. 3), including
dynamic MAC scheduler (Sec. IV-A) and dynamic GM/GV
transmissions (Sec. IV-B). These decisions are later optimized
in Sec. VII.
In a nutshell, the MAC scheduler is in charge of performing
fine-granular uplink/downlink and D2D resource allocation at
each FGTI (i.e., licensed/unlicensed PRB and transmit power
allocation). Also, dynamic GM/GV transmissions using DCC
mode enable controlling the broadcast of GM from O-RUs to
all FLUs, dispersion of local GVs of DPUs across CHUs, and
dispatching of local GVs of CHUs to O-RUs.
A. Dynamic MAC Scheduler for an FL Slice
In this section, we develop one of the first dedicated MAC
scheduler for an FL slice S(k)
b
at O-RU b, which allocates
licensed/unlicensed PRBs to FLUs and transmit power of
FLUs and O-RUs to PRBs tailored to dynamic channel
capacities during FGTIs of global round k. Specifically, the
MAC scheduler operates in three main steps. First, it makes
scheduling decisions (SDs) for downlink/uplink communication
modes at each FGTI (Sec. IV-A1). Second, it allocates PRBs to
the scheduled FLUs (Sec. IV-A2). Third, it allocates transmit
power to PRBs (Sec. IV-A3). We next discuss these steps.
1) Scheduling decisions (SDs): We define three SD variables.
(i) Broadcast scheduling decision variable β↓
b(tx)∈{0, 1},
determining whether O-RU b is scheduled to broadcast the
GM to the recruited FLUs over licensed PRBs of slice S(k)
b
at
FGTI tx. (ii) DPU scheduling decision variable β
↑
u(tx)∈{0, 1},
which determines whether DPU u is scheduled to upload its
LM over unlicensed PRBs at FGTI tx. (iii) CHU scheduling
decision variable β↑
u(tx)∈{0, 1}, specifying whether CHU u
is scheduled to upload its LM over licensed PRBs at FGTI tx.
To ensure proper scheduling, we next impose important SD
constraints (SDCs) on the above SD variables.
Remark 1. In the following, we will face conditions that
can be expressed as if/else statements. Nevertheless, designing
optimal control decisions with respect to if/else statements
pushes the problem to the realm of integer programming, which
is often intractable. Instead, as part of our contributions, we
will demonstrate how to transform these conditions to a set of
continuous non-convex representations (CNRs), enabling us to
develop a tractable approach to optimize the control decisions.

B. Dynamic GM and GV Transmissions
O-RUs GM broadcasting/dispatching. We introduce a
decision variable φb,r(tx)∈[0, 1] to capture the percentage of
the GM that will be broadcast to FLUs over PRB r from O-RU b
at tx. We define the following CNR to ensure that φb,r(tx) > 0
if and only if O-RU b is scheduled for broadcasting the GM:
0 ≤β↓
b(tx) −φb,r(tx) < 1, x∈N (k).
(21)
DPUs local GV dispersion. We utilize a continuous decision
variable ψu,u′,r(tx)∈[0, 1] to capture the percentage of GV of
DPU u∈Ub offloaded to CHU u′∈Ub over unlicensed PRB
r∈Rb at time tx. ψu,u′,r(tx) can be greater than zero only if
PRB r is allocated to DPU u, i.e., ϱu,u′,r(tx)=1. Similarly, if
ϱu,u′,r(tx)=1, DPU u should disperse a fraction of its LM to
CHU u′ over PRB r, i.e., ψu,u′,r(tx) > 0. We capture these
conditions via defining the following CNR:
0 ≤ϱu,u′,r(tx) −ψu,u′,r(tx) < 1, x∈N (k).
(22)
Since DPU u cannot disperse its GV to itself, we also impose
ψu,u,r(tx) = 0,
x∈N (k), ∀r ∈Rb.
(23)
CHUs local GV dispatching. We use a decision variable
ψu,r(tx)∈[0, 1] to denote the percentage of GV of CHU u∈Ub
dispatched to O-RU b over PRB r∈Rb at time tx. Further,
with the same interpretations as (22), we impose
0 ≤ϱu,r(tx) −ψu,r(tx) < 1, x∈N (k).
(24)
V. LATENCY AND ENERGY CONSUMPTION
In this section, we calculate (i) dynamic licensed/unlicensed
data rates (Sec. V-A), (ii) licensed/unlicensed uplink and
downlink latencies (Sec. V-B), and (iii) consumed energy of
FLUs and O-RUs (Sec. V-C). In nutshell, these derivations are
introduced to obtain closed-form expression of the latency and
energy consumption of ML model training with respect to the
dynamic wireless control decisions at each FGTI tx, including
dynamic MAC scheduler (e.g., scheduling decision β↓
b(tx))
and GM/GV transmission decisions (e.g., GM broadcasting
decision φb,r(tx)). These closed-form expressions will be later
integrated into the ML convergence bounds (Sec. VI) and enable
a coherent formulation of DCLM optimization (Sec. VII).

Remark 2. The expressions above and the four propositions
in Sec. V-B2, along with the derivations in Sec. IV, provide
answers to parts (ii) and (iii) of question (Q2) in Sec.
I-C, pertaining to the impact of dynamic wireless control
decisions on ML training latency and energy consumption.
In particular, the above-derived expressions show a nested con-
nection between data rates, transmit powers, PRB allocations,
scheduling decisions, and the occurrence time of D-Events.
These expressions are functions of the downlink and uplink
data rates of FLUs and O-RUs (Sec. V-A), which are in turn
determined by the transmit power decision variables (Sec. V-A)
— themselves functions of the PRB allocation decisions made by
the MAC scheduler (Sec. IV-A3). Further, the MAC scheduler
conducts PRB allocations according to the scheduling decision
variables (Sec. IV-A2), which are in turn dependent on the
occurrence time of D-Events and recruitment indicators of
FLUs (Sec. IV-A1). These results are all later used in Sec. VII
to obtain the optimal orchestration of network elements.
Below, we focus on assessing the convergence behavior of
the global ML model as a means of gauging its performance.
VI. CONVERGENCE ANALYSIS OF DCLM
Before conducting convergence analysis of DCLM, it is imper-
ative to make two standard assumptions [2], [7].
Assumption 1 (β-smoothness of Global/Local Loss Functions).
Local loss function L(k)
u
is β-smooth, ∀u, k: 10
∥∇L(k)
u (ω) −∇L(k)

which results in β-smoothness of global loss function L(k).

set of coefficients {su≥0}u∈Ub,b∈Ω,, where P

Assumption 2 uses X1 and X2 to determine the degree of

FLUs. Larger values of these parameters indicate greater data
heterogeneity, while, in the case of homogeneous/i.i.d datasets,
the minimum values of X1=1 and X2=0 are achieved.

Proof. The proof is carried out in Appendix L.

Below, we explain how the ML-related control decisions
(Sec. III) and dynamic wireless control decisions (Sec. IV)
utilized in DCLM affect the convergence bound in (54).
Interpretation 1: ML-related control decisions. Referring
to (54), term (a) captures the effect of consecutive loss function
gains during ML training. This term is inversely proportional
to ηk, while (c), (d), and (f) are quadratically proportional
to ηk, and (e) is linearly proportional to ηk. Terms (c), (e),
(f) capture the impact of mini-batch sizes Bu(eτ ↓,(k)
b
) used at
the FLUs: these terms become zero when full batch size is
employed (i.e., Bu(eτ ↓,(k)
b
)=|Υu(eτ ↓,(k)
b
)|). The heterogeneity of
the dataset is captured by X2 in (d) and (g), and by X1 in
the condition on ηk. Larger Dataset heterogeneity leads to a
larger upper bound due to (d) and (g). Also, larger local data
dissimilarity implies a larger bound (Θ in (c), (e), and (f)).
Terms (c), (d), (e), (f), and (g) capture the impact of the
number of SGD iterations among FLUs (via ℓ(k)
max and ℓ(k)
n ).
If ℓ(k)
n =1, ∀n, k, (c), (d), and (f) become zero, resulting in
a bound that show 1-epoch distributed ML with non-uniform
SGD sampling and asymmetric FLU recruitment (term (g)).
Interpretation 2: Asymmetric FLU recruitment. The
bound reveals the relationship between FLU recruitment (via
|Υ(eτ ↓,(k))|−|Υs(eτ ↓,(k))|) and both the dataset heterogeneity of
FLUs (captured via X2 in (g)) and their local data dissimilarity
(captured via Θ in (f)): terms (g) and (f) imply that if
X2 and Θ are large, more FLUs with larger dataset must
be recruited. Moreover, if |Υs(eτ ↓,(k))|=|Υ(eτ ↓,(k))|, terms (f)
and (g) become zero, obtaining the bound for full participant
of FLUs. Further, the relationship between FLU recruitment
and model drift is captured via eλ(k)
u
and D(k)
u
in (b), which
implies that recruiting more FLUs reduces the error caused by
model drift. Also, (b) reveals that recruiting FLUs with larger
model drift leads to a smaller bound (i.e., faster convergence).
Interpretation 3: Dynamic dataset and model drift. Terms
(f) and (g) demonstrate that to reduce the error caused by FLU
recruitment, we need to recruit FLUs whose datasets’ size are
larger at time T (k−1) and have a higher growth rate during GM
broadcasting (i.e., for t∈[T (k−1), eτ ↓,(k)
b
]); otherwise |Υ(t)| grows
faster than |Υs(t)|, leading to a larger value of |Υ(t)|−|Υs(t)|
|Υ(t)|
at
time t = eτ ↓,(k) (i.e., the start time of LM training at global
round k) and increased errors caused by terms (f) and (g).
Bound (54), via T (k)−T (k−1) in term (b), also implies that to
mitigate the error caused by model drift (i.e., D(k)
u , ∀u ∈Ub), the
GM broadcasting and FLUs’ GV uploading must be performed
faster as model drift D(k)
u
increases.
Interpretations 2 and 3 answer question ((Q1)) in Sec. I-C.
Interpretation 4: Dynamic wireless control decisions.
As evident from (54), terms (c), (d), (e), (f), and (g) are
functions of the completion time of GM broadcasting, i.e.,
eτ ↓,(k)
b
=T (k−1)+τ ↓,(k)
b
. Also, term (b) is a function of T (k)
and T (k−1) calculated in (44). T (k) and T (k−1) incorporate the
occurrence times of D-events, calculated through propositions
in Sec. V-B2, into bound (54). These propositions are directly
dependent on the dynamic wireless control decisions conducted
at FGTIs (see Sec. IV). Subsequently, bound (54) shows that
DCC communication mode, scheduling decisions and resource
allocation of MAC scheduler, transmit power allocations, and
interference on PRBs experienced from nearby O-RUs directly
12
affect the convergence of DCLM. This interpretation answers
part (i) of question ((Q2)) in Sec. I-C regarding the impact of
dynamic wireless control decisions on ML model accuracy.
Conclusion of Theorem 1. Considering the above inter-
pretations, Theorem 1 provides a deep understanding of the
impact of wireless network conditions on FL. While existing
studies [2], [23], [24] have investigated how wireless control
decisions affect model training latency and energy consumption,
our findings are the first to reveal that such decisions also
significantly influence the convergence behavior of FL. This is
mainly because prior works have focused on static snapshots
of the network, without considering how the network and its
users evolve over time.
In the following theorem, we provide the sufficient conditions
under which convergence of DCLM is guaranteed.

Key interpretations. One of the key findings of Theorem 2
is that for DCLM to converge, we must recruit FLUs whose
cumulative dataset size (i.e., |Υs(eτ ↓,(k))|) satisfies inequal-
ity (57) at each global round k. Another key finding pertains
to sufficient condition C(L). Specifically, if D(k)
u →0, ∀u∈Ub,
the right-hand side of (56) approaches infinity. However, for a
large value of D(k)
u , ∀u∈Ub, the second fraction in the right-
hand side of (56) becomes small. In this situation, for (56) to
hold, as the model drifts of FLUs increase, more FLUs should
be recruited and the communication latencies, integrated in
the left hand side of (56) via (44), should decrease, implying
faster communications.
Corollary 1 in Appendix N provides the convergence bound
of DCLM under sufficient conditions given in Theorem 2.
VII. DCLM NETWORK ORCHESTRATION
In DCLM, we aim to jointly optimize the ML-related control
decisions (Sec. III) and dynamic wireless control decisions
(Sec. IV), which, to our knowledge, is among the most
generic network optimization objectives for FL. Specifically,
we formulate DCLM as the following optimization problem P:

objectives are weighted via non-negative coefficients c1&c2,
respectively. To achieve this tradeoff, P aims to find the optimal

scheduling decisions, PRB and power allocation, GM and GV

Remark 3. Optimization problem P is highly non-trivial to
solve, and it will require numerous mathematical steps to obtain
its solution. To improve the paper’s readability, we do not delve
into all the mathematics involved in solving the problem and
refer the interested reader to Appendix O. Instead, below, we
discuss different aspects of P that are of particular interest.
Challenges Faced in Solving P. The objective function
and constraints of P are highly non-convex and intricate. This
is because of several factors, including (i) terms with negative
signs in the convergence bound (54), (ii) multiplication between
different terms appearing in the objective function and the
constraints of P (e.g., in the convergence bound (54) and
energy consumption calculations in Sec. V-C), (iii) logarithmic
functions in the calculations of the data rates (see Sec. V-A),
(iv) recursive functions such as the global round completion
time (see Sec. V-B2) and scheduling decision constraints (see
Sec. IV-A), (v) and non-convex nested min/max functions
involved in the constraints of P and the calculations of the
communication latencies of DCLM (see Sec. V-B2).
Solution Design. In Appendix O, we draw a resemblance
between P and signomial programming (SP) problems, which
are highly non-convex and, in general, NP-hard. We then intro-
duce and exploit a variety of techniques and approximations
to transform the objective function and the constraints of P
into a set of monomials and posynomials, which transforms
the problem into the format of geometric programming (GP).
Using a logarithmic change of variables, GP, in its standard
form, can be transformed into a convex optimization that can
be efficiently solved with software such as CVXPY [25]. Given
the generality of P, the steps involved in our methodology
are generalizable and can be applied to a wider range of
optimization formulations under the umbrella of network-
aware FL. Nevertheless, they are quite lengthy and are
moved to Appendix O. In a nutshell, the optimization solver
discussed in Appendix O is an iterative solver, which leverages
a combination of the arithmetic-geometric mean inequality
(156), Taylor-power approximation (161), and sum-power

In the following, we conduct numerical evaluations on the
solution of P obtained by our methodology.
VIII. NUMERICAL EVALUATION
To conduct simulations, we use PyTorch [26] and three Nvidia
Tesla V100 GPUs, each equipped with 32 GB VRAM. The
solution of P, derived in (60), is obtained using primal–dual
interior-point method implemented in CVXPY [25]. We con-
sider ML tasks on (i) MNIST [27] dataset and (ii) CIFAR10
[27] dataset. The network configuration and the channel models
used in the simulations are provided in Appendix C.
A. Dynamic Network-Aware DCLM: Ablation Study
Examining P directly is challenging due to the interde-
pendencies between the optimization variables and the bound
in (54). We thus conduct an ablation study to systematically
assess the isolated impacts of key optimization and scaling
variables in P. The results in Secs. VIII-A1-VIII-A6 represent
the averaged outcomes over 10 Monte-Carlo iterations. To avoid
repetition of results, in this section, we only consider MNIST
dataset and use a 1200-kilobit ML model (convolutional neural
network), later utilized in Sec. VIII-B for model training.

Fig. 6: Impact of Θ and X2 on the ML convergence bound over the GP solver
iterations. Larger values of Θ and X2 are associated with slower convergence
of the GP solver and an increased ML convergence upper bound. This is
because convergence bound (54) is directly proportional to Θ and X2 as
reflected in terms (c), (d), (e), (f) and (g) in the bound.
1) Impacts of dynamic model drift: In the left plot of Fig. 4,
we examine the influence of dynamic model drift D(k)
u
on the
time that FLUs are idle. Here, we assume a constant model
drift across all FLUs (i.e., D(k)
u
= D, ∀u, k). The figure shows
that when model drift is high (e.g., D = 0.1), DCLM reduces
the idle time of FLUs in each global round to capture large
changes in data distributions, as governed by term (b) in (54).
Conversely, lower model drifts (e.g., D = 0.0005) allow for
longer idle times for FLUs.
2) Impacts of number of global rounds: In the right plot of
Fig. 4, we illustrate how an increase in the number of global
rounds (K) reduces the ML convergence bound, aligning with
the convergence guarantee of DCLM from Theorem 2.
3) Local data dissimilarity and cross-FLU data heterogene-
ity: In Fig. 5, we vary local data dissimilarity and cross-FLU
data heterogeneity to demonstrate their impact on the number
of SGD iterations and mini-batch size across FLUs. This figure
shows that when local data dissimilarity of FLUs Θ and dataset
heterogeneity across FLUs X2 are small, DCLM maximizes ML
performance by having more local SGD iterations at FLUs
and a smaller mini-batch. Also, as Θ and X2 increase, DCLM
reduces the number of SGD iterations and increases the mini-
batch size of FLUs to avoid local model bias. Further, the
influence of X2 and Θ on the ML convergence upper bound
and the convergence behavior of our GP solver is depicted in
Fig. 6, showing that larger values of X2 and Θ result in slower
convergence of the GP solver and a larger convergence bound.
4) Impacts of MAC scheduler: In the left plot of Fig. 7,
we demonstrate how MAC scheduler can enhance the overall
system performance: an increase in the number of FGTIs
significantly reduces energy consumption of FLUs. This is
because upon increasing the number of FGTIs, fewer FLUs
transfer their models simultaneously on a shared PRB, thereby
reducing interference on communication channels, which, in
turn, reduces energy consumption.
5) Cross FLU data heterogeneity vs FLU recruitment:
Considering the ML convergence sufficient condition C(Υ) in
(57), cross-FLU data heterogeneity, captured via X1, directly
influences the maximum number of FLUs allowed to remain
unrecruited. We show this in the right plot of Fig. 7 by varying
X1 for different total numbers of FLUs in each O-RU (with
Ub ∈[10, 20, 40]). As observed, an increase in X1 reduces the
number of unrecruited FLUs, aligning with (57). Further, an

Fig. 7: Left plot: Increasing the number of FGTIs leads to a substantial
reduction in average energy consumption of FLUs per global round as highers
number of FGTIs allows for scheduling FLUs at non-overlapping FGTIs. Right
plot: An increase in the number of FLUs in each O-RU (|Ub|) allows for a
higher number of unrecruited FLUs. Conversely, an increase in X1 leads to a
reduction in the number of unrecruited FLUs. This is because larger values
of X1 (i.e., higher data heterogeneity) calls for a larger number of recruited
FLUs as reflected in condition C(Υ) in Theorem 2.
increase in the total number of FLUs in each O-RU raises the
maximum allowable number of unrecruited FLUs. Additionally,
in Appendix E, we study the fairness of recruiting FLUs as
CHUs in terms of energy consumption.
6) Impacts of DCC on energy consumption: To demonstrate
the effectiveness of DCC, we evaluate DCLM under different
combinations of the numbers of CHUs and DPUs while keeping
the total number of FLUs constant at 30 (i.e., six FLUs per O-
RU). Specifically, in Fig. 8, we examine six FLU combinations
per O-RU: (a) 1 CHU and 5 DPUs, (c) 2 CHUs and 4 DPUs,
(d) 3 CHUs and 3 DPUs, (e) 4 CHUs and 2 DPUs, (f) 5 CHUs
and 1 DPU, (g) 6 CHUs and 0 DPU. The extreme case with
6 CHUs and no DPU (i.e., the right-most bar) significantly
increases energy consumption. This is primarily because of two
reasons. First, without DPUs communicating over unlicensed
PRBs, the licensed PRBs must be shared among more CHUs,
resulting in increased traffic and interference over the uplink
channels to O-RUs. Second, with the inclusion of DPUs, users
located far from O-RUs can transmit their local models to
nearby CHUs with lower-power D2D communications over
unlicensed PRBs; however, without DPUs, these users must use
more transmit power to transfer their local models to distant
O-RUs. A similar result is observed in the extreme case with
only 1 CHU and 5 DPUs, where all DPUs – regardless of
their closeness to the CHU – must transmit their models to
the selected CHU in D2D communication mode, which is
not efficient upon having long distances between the CHU
and DPUs. The best combination is when there are 3 CHUs
and 3 DPUs, which efficiently distributes the communication
burden over the licensed and unlicensed PRBs via taking the
full advantage of the closeness of DPUs to CHUs and the
proximity of CHUs to the O-RUs.
B. DCLM Model Training Performance
1) Simulation setup: We compare DCLM with three baselines:
FL-EOCD [5], DISCO [15], and AHFLP [4]. FL-EOCD
is an optimization theory-based approach that minimizes
energy consumption through PRB allocation and a D2D
communication model but lacks a MAC scheduler and multi-
channel ML model dispersion. DISCO employs a sequential
wireless resource scheduling policy by establishing a queue
using the Lyapunov optimization framework to determine the

Fig. 9: Energy consumption of DCLM(7), DCLM(1), FL-EOCD, and DISCO
for MNIST (left) and CIFAR10 (right). DCLM variants consume less energy

order in which users transmit their LMs to the server (the
available wireless spectrum is allocated to the users in the
queue, one at a time). AHFLP is also an optimization theory-
based approach and adopts a hierarchical aggregation strategy,
applying different aggregation frequencies l1 and l2 at the edge
and cloud levels, respectively. It performs joint optimization
of l1, l2, bandwidth allocation, and CPU frequency of users,
to minimize FL loss under time and energy constraints, but
does not support D2D communication. We note that FL-EOCD,
DISCO, and AHFLP are not directly comparable to our method:
FL-EOCD and DISCO do not account for communication
interference and consider only a single O-RU, while AHFLP
supports multiple O-RUs but neglects interference. To ensure a
fair comparison, we evaluate DCLM against extended versions of
baselines, adapted to support communication through multiple
O-RUs in the presence of interference. Also, to demonstrate
the effectiveness of the dedicated FL MAC scheduler of DCLM,
we consider two different variants of DCLM, including (i)
DCLM(1): resource allocation is performed at only one FGTI
at the beginning of each global round, (ii) DCLM(7): resource
allocation is performed at 7 FGTIs during each global round.
2) Energy consumption: Fig. 9 shows the energy consump-
tion of DCLM(7), DCLM(1), FL-EOCD, and DISCO for
MNIST (left plot) and CIFAR10 (right plot). As shown, both
DCLM(1) and DCLM(7) consume less energy compared to
FL-EOCD and DISCO. This is because the DCLM variants
effectively minimize interference, thereby reducing energy
consumption, through DCC multi-channel ML model dispersion
over licensed and unlicensed PRBs. Additionally, DCLM(7)
has the lowest energy consumption since, in addition to DCC, it
uses a dedicated MAC scheduler to schedule FLUs at multiple
non-overlapping FGTIs, further reducing interference.

3) FL model accuracy: Tables I&II present the average
ML test accuracy of DCLM(7), DCLM(1), FL-EOCD, and
DISCO on MNIST and CIFAR10 datasets after 40 global
rounds. We employ a Dirichlet distribution [28] with varying
concentration parameters αD ∈{5, 0.5, 0.05} to partition the
MNIST and CIFAR-10 datasets among FLUs, allowing us
to study how different levels of data heterogeneity affect
DCLM’s performance. Here, αD=0.05 introduces extreme data
heterogeneity [28]. Fig. 13 in Appendix G visualizes class
distributions under each αD value. In this simulation, following
the first realization of FLUs’ datasets, in the subsequent global
rounds, FLUs obtain a new dataset of size dictated by the piece-
wise differential equation in (61). The second column of both
tables shows the average accuracy and standard deviation across
20 runs of DCLM(7). For DCLM(1), AHFLP, FL-EOCD, and
DISCO, each column provides: (i) the average accuracy and
standard deviation and (ii) the P-value from the Wilcoxon
test11 comparing DCLM(7) with the respective algorithm at
a significance level of 0.05 [29]. Referring to Tables I&II,
DCLM(7) statistically outperforms DCLM(1), AHFLP, FL-
EOCD, and DISCO for the three levels of data heterogeneity
on both MNIST and CIFAR10. This is because, in DCLM(7),
FLUs consume significantly less energy and can participate
in more global rounds. For instance, AHFLP and FL-EOCD
consume twice as much energy as DCLM(7) (see Fig. 9).
This highlights the effectiveness of (i) multi-channel model
transmission over licensed/unlicensed PRBs using the DCC,
and (ii) scheduling FLUs at non-overlapping FGTIs through
the dedicated MAC scheduler of DCLM.
IX. CONCLUSION AND FUTURE WORK
We introduced DCLM, incorporating dynamic channels and time-
varying FLUs’ datasets into FL. DCLM introduces dynamic
ML and wireless control decisions, which are optimized via
11The Wilcoxon test is a non-parametric statistical test used to compare
paired samples and assess whether their population distributions differ
significantly [29]. In this paper, it evaluates the significance of differences in
ML test accuracy between DCLM(7) and the other three algorithms.
16
introducing dedicated FL MAC schedulers over O-RAN and
efficient implementation of DCC mode. This was carried
out through formulating a non-convex optimization problem
and proposing a generalizable solution methodology. Our
theoretical analysis and analytical optimization solver form
the baseline for studying dynamic resource allocation and
MAC scheduler for FL over O-RAN. Specifically, with AI/ML
techniques integrated into O-RAN intelligent controllers (i.e.,
RICs), we expect them to be compared to our analytical
approach. Furthermore, a comprehensive set of future works
were presented in the paper and Appendix B.

APPENDIX B
FUTURE/OPEN RESEARCH DIRECTIONS
Remark 4 (Multiple FL Services and Concurrent Task/Service Processing). Our model can be easily extended to cover
coexistence of multiple FLSs and other types of services, such as URLLC and mMTC, studying which is left as future work.
Remark 5 (FL Service Competition and Unbalanced Execution). This paper builds the theoretical pillars for an unexplored
research direction on modeling the coexistence of multiple FLSs in the system with conflicts of interest, which have to compete
with each other to recruit FLUs and acquire network resources (e.g., wireless spectrum) from different O-RUs [30]. This
situation may further lead to overcrowdedness in specific O-RUs that contain low-cost (e.g., in terms of recruitment cost)
and high-quality (e.g., in terms of the quality of taken images for image-oriented FLSs) FLUs [30]. Further modeling and
investigations of such scenarios are left as future work.
Remark 6 (Stochastic System Analysis). If either users’ locations or their computation capabilities are stochastic, the values
of Ψ(D↓,(k)
u
), Ψ(D
↚
,(k)
u
), Ψ(D
↑,(k)
u
), Ψ(D
↛
,(k)
u
), Ψ(D↑,(k)
u
), and Ψ(D
↛
,(k)
u
) will become stochastic, which calls for stochastic
dynamic optimal control strategies studying of which are left as future work.
Remark 7 (Non-Deterministic MAC Scheduler Design). As mentioned in Sec II-B, for deterministic systems, the resource
requirements (i.e., PRBs and transmit power requirements) of FLS slices are precisely known as the channel conditions change
at known discrete-time instants, which can be accomplished through the channel quality estimation procedure commonly used
in contemporary cellular systems. This enables both provisioning wireless resources (i.e., RAN slice scaling) to FLS slices
and allocating these resources by MAC schedulers of each slice to their users according to the users’ exact requirements at
each FGTI. However, if the system is stochastic (e.g., abrupt environment changes, stochastic arrival/departure of FLUs, and
stochastic availability of FLUs’ computation resources) the future state of the system becomes uncertain, and the resource
requirements of the slices cannot be accurately determined. In such cases, resource allocation by MAC schedulers and slice
scaling must be performed at different time scales. This necessitates a coarse granular time scale for slice scaling operation
comprising multiple FGTIs. This situation requires stochastic analysis and ML-assisted methods to predict the future state of
the system at predetermined time instants, studying which is a promising research direction and left for future work.
Remark 8 (Considering More Comprehensive O-RAN Features in the Design). In general, an O-RAN slice consists of (i)
a set of PRBs, (ii) transmit power, and (iii) a set of virtual network functions (VNFs) executed on computation resources of
O-DUs and O-CUs to process the traffic flow of PRBs. In this paper, we focus on a scenario in which an FLS slice consists of
unlicensed/licensed PRBs and transmit power. Considering scenarios with VNFs are left for future work. In particular, designing
of VNFs will require mathematical modeling of the traffic flow over the fronthaul and midhaul of the network.
Remark 9 (Convergence Acceleration under D2D Data Transmission). In situations where privacy concerns are not paramount
for data collected at the FLUs (such as visual data of traffic signs gathered by smart cars), there is potential for data transmission
between FLUs over O-RAN via D2D communications. In such cases, optimizing data transmission is essential to minimize data
heterogeneity across FLUs. This necessitates the DCML MAC scheduler to handle data transmission while considering system
dynamics alongside ML model parameter transmission, requiring dynamic control decision variables. Exploring these scenarios
is a subject for future research.
Remark 10 (Convergence Acceleration under D2D Model Consensus). Building on Remark 9, we can also consider the
possibility of model censuses among DPUs through D2D communications. In this framework, DPUs can create localized
networks and conduct local consensus (e.g., through distributed averaging mechanisms used in decentralized FL literature) to
de-bias their local models, and then only a small fraction of them engage in model transmission to CHUs. Further, CHUs can
also create such localized networks and engage in D2D-assisted model consensus, after which only a small fraction of CHUs
will engage in uplink transmissions to the O-RU. Detailed modeling and formulation of these aspects are deferred to future
work.
Remark 11 (Asynchronous Model Training). In this study, our focus is on synchronized FL training, the most widely recognized
structure for ML model training in FL. In this setup, the server waits for models from a large subset of FLUs before aggregating
them to form a global model. However, one can envision a more flexible architecture where the server updates the global model
more rapidly. For instance, upon receiving each model from every FLU, it could immediately update the global model. Such
update rules shift the focus to asynchronous FL over O-RAN requiring redesigning dedicated FL orchestrator system over
O-RAN slices, a topic left for future investigation.
Remark 12 (Distributed Multi-Modal Learning). In this paper, similar to the prevailing approach in FL research, our focus
was on scenarios where FLUs possess data in a single modality. However, FLUs can gather data across multiple modalities
(e.g., video, image, and audio) in dynamic environments, necessitating appropriate scheduling strategies and data processing
load balancing. Exploring such scenarios will extend the design to the domain of multi-modal FL over O-RAN, a topic that
20
remains largely unexplored. Notably, the data modality collected by users can influence recruitment decisions and network
operations. Investigating these scenarios presents intriguing avenues for future research.
21

Fig. 11: Plain DCLM achieves the lowest overall energy consumption compared to both fixed-CHUs DCLM and fair DCLM. Additionally, fixed-CHUs
DCLM results in the highest total energy consumption and the most unfair energy consumption of CHUs vs DPUs due to its inability to adapt the selection of
FLUs as CHUs to the evolving network conditions. An interesting insight is that while fair DCLM achieves the most balanced energy consumption between
CHUs and DPUs (i.e., the fairest energy consumption among the FLUs), it does not lead to the most energy-efficient operation of the overall system. In other
words, the superior energy efficiency of Plain DCLM is achieved by slightly compromising the fairness of energy consumption between DPUs and CHUs.
In this section, we examine the fairness of DCLM by comparing DCLM(7), here referred to as plain DCLM, with two
modified versions. The first is Fixed-CHUs DCLM, where the optimization is constrained to select the same set of FLUs as
CHUs across all global rounds. The second is fair DCLM, which incorporates a fairness term – defined as the modified standard
deviation of the energy consumption of FLUs – into the objective function of the optimization problem P. Mathematically, we
add the following term to the objective function of P:

minimization of this term results in CHUs and DPUs having the same level of energy consumption (i.e., only when the overall
energy consumption of CHUs is equivalent to the overall energy consumption of DPUs, which makes them both equivalent to
the average energy consumption of FLUs, term F would be equal to zero).
We would like to emphasize that the default version of our optimization, i.e., plain DCLM, naturally results in
changing/flipping the CHUs over time since the goal of the optimization is to optimize the selection of CHUs to minimize the
overall energy consumption of the system, while taking into account the time-varying channels of FLUs.
Fig. 11 illustrates that plain DCLM achieves the lowest overall energy consumption compared to both fixed-CHUs DCLM
and fair DCLM, while slightly compromising the fairness of energy consumption between DPUs and CHUs. In this context,
fairness implies that the average energy consumption per round for both DPUs and CHUs is nearly equal (i.e., although CHUs
conduct uplink transmissions to the O-RUs, which are often more energy demanding than D2D communications conducted by
DPUs, the overall energy consumption of CHUs is kept at the same level of DPUs).
The reduction in the overall energy consumption of FLUs achieved via plain DCLM is primarily because the channel
quality of FLUs fluctuates over FGTIs, and plain DCLM minimizes the overall energy consumption of FLUs – dictated by
terms (c) in the objective function of P – by dynamically selecting FLUs as CHUs with favorable channel quality to both
DPUs and O-RUs in each global round (see Appendix C for details on the dynamic channel model used in this paper).
Note that the total energy consumption of FLUs in fair DCLM is slightly higher than in plain DCLM, as the optimization
problem favors balancing the total energy consumption of FLUs with the standard deviation of their energy consumption (i.e.,
it favors having equal amounts of energy consumption over DPUs and CHUs although this may result in an increase in the
24
overall energy consumption). Interestingly, this highlights that inducing fairness in terms of energy consumption across the
FLUs does not always align with the optimal energy-efficient solution for the overall system. Additionally, the figure shows
that fixed-CHUs DCLM results in the highest total energy consumption and the most unfair strategy for selecting CHUs in
terms of energy consumption, due to its inability to adapt the selection of FLUs as CHUs to the evolving network conditions.

APPENDIX F
THE EVOLUTION OF RAN: FROM DISTRIBUTED RAN TO O-RAN
RAN technologies have evolved significantly since their invention as traditional RANs to accommodate the ever-changing
wireless network demands (Fig. 12 depicts the evolution of RAN technologies) [32]. In particular, the 3GPP 4G LTE RAN
(Long-Term Evolution Radio Access Network), e.g., distributed RAN (D-RAN) shown in the left part of Fig. 12, is a traditional,
centralized network architecture that relies on proprietary hardware and software from specific vendors, focusing on enhanced
mobile broadband (eMBB) services. It employs a tightly integrated infrastructure with base stations (eNBs) communicating
directly with the core network. Specifically, in D-RAN, the functionalities of the 3GPP protocol stack – that is, (i) physical
(PHY) low in the remote radio head (RRH), and (ii) PHY-high, medium access control (MAC), radio link control (RLC), packet
data convergence protocol (PCDP), and radio resource control (RRC) in the base band unit (BBU) – are placed at each base
station near the users. While effective for 4G networks, this architecture is costly (i.e., in terms of management and energy
consumption costs) and lacks the scalability required for 5G-and-beyond networks [32]. Compared with D-RAN, cloud RAN
(C-RAN), middle part of Fig. 12, exploits cloud computing services and realizes the sharing of processing resources by pooling
BBUs into centralized units named BBU pools, which can effectively reduce energy consumption and management costs [32].
However, C-RAN requires a tremendous amount of fronthaul capacity from the BBU pools to the RRHs [32].
In contrast to the aforementioned two traditional RAN architectures, in O-RAN, right part of Fig. 12, the elements of the
base station are disaggregated into three distinct components: (i) radio unit (O-RU), responsible for the PHY-low and radio
frequency (RF) functionalities, (ii) distributed unit (O-DU), responsible for the PHY-high, MAC, and RLC functionalities, and
(iii) centralized unit (O-CU) responsible for the remainder of the 3GPP stack (i.e., RRC, service data adaption protocol (SDAP),
and PCDP) [10]. O-RAN also introduces the concept of non-real-time (non-RT) RAN intelligent controllers (RICs) and near-RT
RICs which orchestrate RAN operations (e.g., resource allocation and interference management) via software applications called
rApps and xApps [10].
The O-RAN components (i.e., O-RUs, O-DUs, O-CUs, and RICs) interact with each other via standard open interfaces,
facilitating interoperability between network elements from different manufacturers. Specifically, each O-CU provides RRC,
SDAP, and PCDP functionalities for multiple O-DUs through standard F1 interface. Each O-DU is also associated with multiple
O-RUs via standard open fronthaul interface and provides PHY-high, MAC, and RLC functionalities for them. Correspondingly,
each O-RU delivers network connection for users of various network services (i.e., FL, mobile broadband, ultra-reliable
low-latency communications, and massive machine-type communications). The interaction between RICs and other elements of
O-RAN is explained below.
O-RAN intelligent orchestration system. In O-RAN, data (e.g., key performance measurements (KPMs) [10]), generated by
the RAN nodes, i.e., O-CUs, O-DUs, and O-RUs, stream periodically via the O1 interface to the virtual event streaming (VES).
26
The data collected by VES is utilized by rApps in non-RT RICs to establish policies for wireless network management. These
policies are packaged into xApps and instantiated on demand on the near-RT RICs, utilized to fine-tune the behavior of RAN
via performing near-RT RAN operations, such as mobility management and RAN slicing. In particular, RAN slicing refers to
partitioning the physical RAN resources (e.g., radio resources), governed by a network operator, into various isolated logical
slices, each leased to a virtual network operator (VNO). VNOs tailor their RAN slices to meet their service requirements by
adopting appropriate RAN control strategies (e.g., MAC scheduling and mobility management). Here, a MAC scheduler is an
essential part of O-DU, where real-time radio resources allocation is performed according to the current network conditions
[10]. MAC scheduler also enforces the necessary QoS requirements for the users’ various services.
In traditional closed RANs (e.g., 3GPP LTE D-RAN), network operator cannot form/alter the network functionalities (e.g.,
a rigid black box MAC scheduler in 4G is utilized for all the users’ services). To address this limitation, O-RAN offers a
flexible infrastructure for designing dedicated functionalities for each virtual RAN slice, a feature referred to as programmability.
In DCLM, we take advantage of the programmability feature of O-RAN to design a dedicated MAC scheduler for dynamic
resource allocation in FL. This scheduler, deployed at the MAC layer (as labeled DCLM MAC scheduler in the right part of
Fig. 12), schedules CHUs and DPUs across different fine-granular time instants (FGTIs). By dynamically allocating licensed
and unlicensed PRBs to CHUs and DPUs at different FGTIs, the DCLM MAC scheduler effectively reduces interference across
FLUs, enhancing resource utilization and reducing energy consumption. It is worth mentioning that D2D communications have
been proposed as a part of O-RAN [33]; however, this work is the first to take a unique advantage of D2D communications in
O-RAN for FL. In particular, in this work, D2D communications is used between the DPUs and CHUs to save the licensed
spectrum, mitigate the interference, and reduce the energy consumption and latency of model aggregations in FL over O-RAN.
APPENDIX G
DATA HETEROGENEITY VISUALIZATION
Fig. 13 illustrates how varying degrees of data heterogeneity, modeled through the Dirichlet distribution parameter αD,
influence the class distribution across five O-RUs and their covered 30 FLUs. The color-coded bars represent the relative class
distribution per O-RU, with the vertical axis indicating the number of samples and the horizontal axis covering normalized class
frequency. The three sub-figures correspond to different αD values: (i) Left subfigure (Low Heterogeneity, αD = 5): Each O-RU
and its FLs receive a relatively balanced mix of all ten classes, indicating a nearly i.i.d. (independent and identically distributed)
setting. (ii) Middle subfigure (Moderate Heterogeneity, αD = 0.5): The data becomes more skewed across O-RUs and their
FLUs. This setup reflects a moderately non-IID scenario. (iii) Right subfigure (Extreme Heterogeneity, αD = 0.05): Most
O-RUs and FLUs receive data samples from only a small subset of the classes, showcasing an extremely non-IID distribution.

APPENDIX O
TRANSFORMING THE OPTIMIZATION PROBLEM OF DCML
In the following, we provide a tractable technique to solve P through utilizing geometric programming (GP) [36]. Our
approach relies on a set of approximations and constraint modifications to solve the problem in an iterative manner, which
enjoys strong convergence guarantees.
A. Geometric Programming (GP)
Consider the following two key definitions: O-A
Definition 3 (Monomial). A monomial is a function f : Rn

m=1 dmyα(1)

Accordingly, a standard GP is a non-convex optimization problem defined as minimizing a posynomial subject to inequality
constraints on posynomials (and monomials) and equality constraints on monomials [36]:

Nevertheless, P does not admit the format of GP due to several factors which violate the definition of posynomials, including
(i) negative terms in the convergence bound (54), (ii) logarithmic functions in the calculations of the data transmission rates (see
Sec. V-A), (iii) recursive functions such as the global round completion time (see Sec. V-B2) and scheduling decision constraints
(see Sec. IV-A), (iv) and non-convex nested min/max functions involved in the constraints of P and the calculations of the
communication latencies of DCLM (see Sec. V-B2). In order to address these violations, we utilize a series of approximations
and constraint modifications. Among these techniques, we frequently use the arithmetic-geometric mean inequality presented in
the following lemma to upper bound a posynomial with a larger monomial in value.
Lemma 2 (Arithmetic-geometric mean inequality [37]). Consider a posynomial function g(y) = Pi′
i=1 ui(y), where ui(y)
is a monomial, ∀i. The following inequality holds:
g(y) ≥ˆg(y) ≜
i′
Y
i=1
ui(y)
αi(z)
αi(z)
,
(156)
where αi(z) = ui(z)/g(z), ∀i, and z > 0 is a fixed point.
In the following sections, we transform the objective function and constraints of optimization problem P into standard
GP format. Specifically, first, we focus on transforming data transmission rates (Sec. O-B) and computation/communication
latencies (Sec. O-C), which appear in all terms of the objective function and different constraints of the optimization problem
P (see (60)). Next, we transform energy consumption equations into standard GP format (Sec. O-D). We then present the
transformation of the objective function of P (Sec. O-E). Finally, we transform constraints of P into standard GP format. This
includes transforming (i) sufficient conditions of ML convergence (Sec. O-E), (ii) scheduling decisions constraints (Sec. O-F),
(iii) PRB/Power allocations constraints (Sec. O-G), and (iv) GM/GPs transmissions constraints (Sec. O-H).
Note that, for brevity of notation, we refer to the vector of all decision variables as v.
12Rn
++ denotes the strictly positive quadrant of n-dimensional Euclidean space.

I. Pseudo Code of Our GP Optimization Solver
Algorithm 1: Proposed Signomial programming and GP-based optimization solver for problem P
input
: Convergence criterion.
1 Set the algorithm iteration counter to be ℓ= 0.
2 Choose an initial feasible point as the starting point v[0].
3 Obtain the highlighted approximations, indicated by green boxes, from page 52 to page 133, using the current solution v[ℓ].
4 Use those approximations instead of their original constraints in Problem P, which results in a GP problem.
5 Utilize logarithmic variable transformation to convert the resultant GP problem into a convex problem, following the instructions in Appendix O-A.
6 Increment ℓby one, i.e., ℓ= ℓ+ 1.
7 Utilize state-of-the-art convex optimization solver framework (such as CVXPY [25]) to solve the obtained convex problem and derive v[ℓ].
8 if successive solutions v[ℓ−1] and v[ℓ] fail to satisfy the defined convergence criterion then
9
Go to line 3 and re-iterate over the steps using the most recent solution v[ℓ].
10
else
11
Set the most recent solution as the final solution of the problem v⋆= v[ℓ].
Proposition 5 (Convergence of the Optimization Solver). Our GP-based solver (i.e. Algorithm 1 presented in Appendix. O-I)
generates a sequence of solutions for P using the approximations (a-i) arithmetic-geometric mean inequality (156), (a-ii)
Taylor-power approximation (161), and (a-iii) sum-power approximations min{A, B} ≈(A−p + B−p)−1
p and max{A, B} ≈
(Ap + Bp)−1
p . Under tight approximations of (a-ii) and (a-iii), these solutions (i.e., v⋆) converge to the Karush–Kuhn–Tucker
(KKT) conditions of P upon using (a-i).
Proof. Under the tight approximations of (a-ii) and (a-iii), Algorithm 1 leads to an inner approximation [38] of P. It is thus
sufficient to examine the three characteristics mentioned in [38] for our solver to prove the convergence, which can be done
using the methods in [39] and omitted for brevity.
■
135
APPENDIX P
COMPLEXITY ANALYSIS AND FEASIBILITY OF DCLM
A. Complexity Analysis of DCLM
As we demonstrated in Appendix O, our optimization problem (P), presented in (60), can be transformed to a GP optimization
problem. This GP can be further reformulated as a convex optimization problem through a logarithmic transformation [36],
[40]. CVXPY then leverages interior-point methods (IPMs) to solve the convex reformulation of this GP problem by iteratively
updating the solution using the Karush-Kuhn-Tucker (KKT) conditions (see Proposition 5 in Appendix O). In this approach, each
iteration will involve solving a Newton system derived from the KKT conditions, which requires solving a linear system with a
complexity of approximately O(n2), where n is the size of the problem, i.e., the sum of the number of variables and constraints
(for details refer to [40]). Subsequently, the number of iterations required for IPMs in convex optimization to achieve a desired
solution accuracy of ϵ > 0 is given by O
 √n log
  1
ϵ

[40]. As a result, the complexity of solving GP optimization problem
using CVXPY is O
 n2.5 log
  1
ϵ

, which is polynomial with respect to n making it a good candidate for large problem sizes.
Further, note that implementing DCLM does not need following all the detailed mathematical steps presented in Appendix O;
instead, it relies solely on the closed-form expressions, which are highlighted in green boxes throughout Appendix O, ensuring
that implementation of DCLM is mathematically tractable.
B. Feasibility of Implementing DCLM in Real-World Scenarios
Feasibility of realizing our proposed DCLM method in real world scenarios can be investigated from two angles. First, as we
discuss in Appendix Sec. A of Appendix P, the complexity of solving optimization problem P using CVXPY is polynomial,
making DCLM computationally feasible. It is worth noting that modeling an engineering optimization problem using geometric
programming (GP), particularly for large-scale wireless networks entailing large numbers of variables and constraints, is widely
exercised [36].
Second, implementing DCLM does not require conducting of all the detailed mathematical steps presented in Appendix O;
instead, it relies solely on the closed-form expressions, which are highlighted in green boxes throughout Appendix O, ensuring
that implementation of DCLM is practical. Please note that the detailed mathematical steps in Appendix O are intended to
provide a comprehensive theoretical reference to demonstrate how such closed-form expressions can be obtained for the complex
optimization problems of the form considered in this paper. This step-by-step analysis can be further used as a reference for
future works.
